Kael spun around. His torch illuminated the tunnel behind him. There was nothing there.


The whisper became a word.


"Kael."


He knew that voice. It was his mother. His mother had died fifty years ago. He had held her hand as she passed. He had buried her himself.


"You are not real," he said.


The whisper laughed.


"I am as real as you."


Kael ran.


He did not know where he was going. He did not care. He just ran. His torch flickered. The shadows danced. The walls blurred.


He ran until his legs burned. He ran until his lungs ached. He ran until he could run no more.


He collapsed against a wall. He looked back. The tunnel was empty.


He closed his eyes.


"Wake up," the whisper said.


Kael opened his eyes.


He was not in the tunnel anymore. He was in his mother's house. The house where he had grown up. The house that had been destroyed a hundred years ago.


His mother was sitting by the fire. She was young. She was beautiful. She was alive.


"Sit with me, Kael," she said. "I have missed you."


Kael walked to the fire. He sat across from her. He knew this was not real. He knew this was a trap. He could not resist.


"I missed you too, Mother."


She smiled. "Stay with me. You do not have to go back. You do not have to be a scout. You do not have to be Noctari. You can just be my son."


Kael felt tears on his cheeks. "I want to stay."


"Then stay."


A hand touched his shoulder. Cold. Bony. Wrong.


Kael opened his eyes. He was back in the tunnel. The torch had gone out. He was in total darkness.


The hand squeezed.


"Stay," the whisper said.


Kael tried to move. He could not. His body was frozen. His arms would not lift. His legs would not run. His mouth would not speak.


The hand moved from his shoulder to his neck. The fingers were long. Too long. They wrapped around his throat.


"Stay with me, Kael. I am so lonely. I have been alone for so long."


Kael saw it. Not with his eyes. With his mind. A shape in the darkness. Tall. Thin. Made of shadow and frost. Its face had no features. No eyes. No nose. No mouth. But it was smiling.


Kael had heard stories. The Gloom Weavers. The dreams that became self aware. They fed on loneliness. They wrapped themselves around isolated minds and whispered.


He was alone. He had been alone for three days. He had been alone for thirty years.


"I am not lonely," Kael lied.


The Gloom Weaver laughed. The sound was like ice cracking.


"You are the loneliest soul I have ever tasted."


Kael closed his eyes. He thought of his mother. He thought of her real face. Old. Weathered. Dying. He thought of her last words.


"Live, Kael. Do not waste your life in the dark."


He opened his eyes.


"I choose to live."


The Gloom Weaver screamed. The sound was agony. It released him. It retreated into the darkness.


Kael gasped. He could move again. He fumbled for his flint. He lit a new torch.


The tunnel was empty. The whisper was gone.


He sat against the wall and wept. Not for his mother. Not for the Gloom Weaver. For himself.


He had spent thirty years in the dark. He had spent thirty years alone. He had spent thirty years serving the Shadow Council, hunting enemies, spying on neighbors, killing strangers.


He had never lived.


Kael did not complete his mission. He did not find the dwarves. He walked out of the Underdark. He walked to the surface. He walked to the nearest Jin settlement. He asked for asylum.


The Jin were surprised. A Noctari defector was rare. They accepted him.


He spent the rest of his life on the surface. He learned to farm. He learned to love. He learned to be happy.


He never returned to the Underdark. He never heard the whisper again.


But sometimes, late at night, when the wind was cold and the house was quiet, he thought he felt a hand on his shoulder.


He always told himself it was his imagination.


He was never sure.


---


### ONE SHOT EIGHTEEN: THE LAST LETTER


**Era:** The Vallian Colonization Era, approximately 1800 AW
**Location:** The border between Vallia and the Jin territories of Orentis
**Characters:** Caius, a Vallian legionnaire, and Lin, a Jin spy


Caius had been a soldier for twelve years. He had seen battle. He had seen death. He had seen the worst that men could do to each other. He thought he was ready for anything.


He was not ready for her.


He met her in a tavern on the border. A dusty town with no name, caught between the Vallian Empire and the Jin territories. Neither side claimed it. Both sides used it. Smugglers, spies, deserters, and dreamers all passed through.


She was sitting in the corner. Dark hair. Dark eyes. A silver pendant around her neck. She was reading a book. Not a spy's tradecraft. Not a coded message. A book of poetry.


Caius sat across from her. "Is that seat taken?"


She looked up. Her eyes were calm. "It is now."


He ordered wine. She ordered tea. They did not speak for an hour. They simply sat together, reading, drinking, existing in the same space.


It was the most peaceful hour of his life.


The second time he saw her, she was running.


A patrol of Vallian soldiers was chasing her through the streets. She had been caught near the garrison. They thought she was a spy. She probably was.


Caius did not think. He acted.


He stepped into the alley. He grabbed her arm. He pulled her through a doorway and into a cellar. He closed the door behind them. The soldiers ran past.


They crouched in the darkness. He could hear her breathing. Fast. Shallow. Afraid.


"Why did you help me?" she whispered.


"Because you were reading poetry," he said.


She laughed. It was a small sound. Almost a sob.


"My name is Lin," she said.


"Caius."


They stayed in the cellar until midnight. They talked. They learned each other's stories. He was a legionnaire, tired of war, counting the days until his retirement. She was a Jin spy, tired of secrets, counting the days until she could go home.


"I have no home," she said. "The Jin do not have homes. We have safe houses."


"That sounds lonely."


"It is."


He took her hand. She did not pull away.


They met in secret for a year. Once a month, sometimes more, they found each other in the border town. They walked through the market. They shared meals. They talked about everything except the things that would tear them apart.


She never told him her missions. He never told her his patrol routes. They did not ask. They did not want to know.


"We are enemies," she said one night. They were lying in a hayloft, looking up at the stars through a gap in the roof.


"Are we?" he asked.


"My people fight your people. My spies undermine your empire. My government wants to see Vallia crumble."


"And my legions patrol your borders. My generals plan campaigns against your territory. My emperor wants to absorb your lands."


She turned to look at him. "Then why are we here?"


He turned to look at her. "Because I love you."


She closed her eyes. Tears slipped down her cheeks.


"I love you too."


The third time he saw her, she was dying.


A Vallian patrol had caught her crossing the border. They had not captured her. They had shot her. She was lying in the grass, blood soaking through her dark clothes, her silver pendant stained red.


Caius ran to her. He knelt in the mud. He pressed his hands against the wound. The blood kept flowing.


"Lin," he said. "Lin, stay with me."


She opened her eyes. They were glassy. Far away.


"Caius," she whispered. "There is a letter. In my coat. Take it."


He reached into her coat. His hands were shaking. He found the letter. It was addressed to him.


"I wrote it last week," she said. "In case I did not survive. I wanted you to know."


"Know what?"


"That I was going to leave the Jin. That I was going to find you. That I was going to ask you to run away with me."


Caius wept. He had not wept since he was a child.


"Lin, I would have said yes. I would have run anywhere with you."


She smiled. "I know."


She died in his arms. The sun set. The stars came out. He held her until she grew cold.


He read the letter that night, by candlelight, in the same tavern where they had first met.


"Caius. If you are reading this, I am dead. I am sorry. I wanted to tell you in person, but I was a coward. I am a spy. I have spent my whole life hiding. I did not know how to stop.


I love you. I have loved you since the moment you sat across from me and asked if my seat was taken. You were so earnest. So honest. So unlike anyone I had ever met.


I am leaving the Jin. I have already told my handlers. They will not let me go easily. I may not survive. But I have to try.


Meet me at the border. One month from today. I will be there. I will be the woman who is not hiding anymore.


If I do not come, know that I tried. Know that I loved you. Know that you were the best thing in a life full of shadows.


Yours, Lin."


Caius folded the letter. He put it in his breast pocket, over his heart.


He did not go to the border. There was no point. Lin was dead.


He finished his service. He retired from the legions. He bought a farm on the border, as close to the Jin territories as he could get.


He never remarried. He never had children. He grew old alone.


Every night, he read the letter. Every night, he wept.


When he died, the letter was found clutched in his hands. The paper was worn thin from decades of reading. The ink was faded. The words were still legible.


"I love you. I have loved you since the moment you sat across from me."


The letter was buried with him. The farm was abandoned. The border town eventually disappeared.


But the story survived. Travelers told it. Lovers remembered it. The tragic love between a Vallian soldier and a Jin spy.


They never had a future. They had a letter. That was enough.


---


### ONE SHOT FOUR: THE MOTHER'S PROMISE


**Era:** The Kingdom Period, approximately 4500 AW
**Location:** The Misty Valleys of Orentis
**Character:** Li Hua, a mother of House Jin


Li Hua's son was dead.


The message came at midnight. A Jin courier, silent as shadow, handed her a sealed scroll. She read it once. She read it twice. She set it on the table and stared at the wall.


Her son, Jian, had been a spy. He had been operating in Vallia, gathering intelligence on the Imperial Senate. He had been discovered. He had been executed. His body had been thrown into the Vallian River.


No. Not executed. Murdered.


Li Hua was not a spy. She was not a warrior. She was a merchant. She traded silk and tea. She had never killed anyone. She did not know how to kill.


But she knew how to wait.


She did not cry. She did not scream. She did not tell her husband. She put the scroll in the fire and watched it burn. She went to bed. She slept. In the morning, she made tea.


The Vallian who had killed her son was named Decimus. He was a senator. He was wealthy. He was powerful. He had a villa on the silver coast, a wife, two daughters, and a network of spies who reported to him. He had ordered the execution of at least forty Jin agents. He was proud of his work.


Li Hua began to plan.


She did not hire an assassin. Assassins were expensive and unreliable. She did not poison him. Poisons were traceable. She did not confront him. That would be suicide.


She became his friend.


It took three years.


She learned his habits. She learned his weaknesses. She learned that he loved wine, that he was unfaithful to his wife, that he was afraid of the dark. She learned that he had nightmares about the people he had killed. He did not dream of their faces. He dreamed of their silence.


Li Hua sent him gifts. Fine silk from Orentis. Rare tea from the mountain valleys. A painting of a sunset over the sea. He accepted them. He sent back thanks. He invited her to his villa.


She went.


She brought more gifts. She smiled. She laughed at his jokes. She listened to his stories. He never knew who she was.


One night, after three years of patience, Decimus got drunk. He talked about his work. He bragged about the spies he had caught. He mentioned her son.


"You killed him," Li Hua said.


Decimus looked at her. His eyes were glassy. "What?"


"Jin. The spy. You killed him. He was my son."


Decimus reached for his sword. He was too slow. Too drunk. Too old.


Li Hua had brought one gift that was not a gift. A small needle. No poison. No magic. Just sharp steel.


She killed him quickly. She had practiced on pigs. A pig's throat is similar to a man's. She did not think about her son. She did not think about anything. She just did it.


She left the villa. She walked to the coast. She took a ship back to Orentis. She went home. She made tea.


Her husband asked where she had been.


"Traveling," she said. "For business."


He did not ask more. He knew better.


Li Hua never told anyone what she had done. The Jin never found out. The Vallians never found out. Decimus's death was ruled a robbery gone wrong.


She lived to be ninety years old. She died in her sleep. Her last word was not a word. It was a smile.


Her son's name was Jian. It means strong. She never told him she loved him. She hoped he knew.


---


## PART FIVE: THE GODS AND THE DAMNED


---


### ONE SHOT FIFTEEN: THE BROTHERS' ARGUMENT


**Era:** The Early Expansion Era, approximately 8000 BA
**Location:** The Aurealis, the realm of Solari
**Character:** Solari and Valerius


Solari stood at the window of his golden palace and watched the mortals below. The Elves were building their first cities. The Dwarves were digging their first mines. The Orcs were fighting their first wars. The world was young. The Aether was fresh. The silence of the Great Aether was still new.


Valerius entered without knocking. He always entered without knocking. He was the only god who dared.


"Brother," Valerius said. "We need to talk."


Solari did not turn. "We are talking."


"The Elves. Your son Aurelian is among them. He is young. He is powerful. He is destined for greatness. But the other Elves are not like him. They are ambitious. They are proud. They are reaching for things they should not reach."


Solari turned. His golden eyes blazed. "You speak of House Umbra."


"I speak of all of them. They are your children, Solari. They are mine as well. I created them. I love them. But I see the darkness in their hearts."


Solari walked to his throne. He sat. He gestured for Valerius to sit.


"They are not dark yet, brother. They are curious. Curiosity is not evil."


"It becomes evil when it reaches for the Void."


Solari was silent. He knew Valerius was right. He did not want to admit it.


"What would you have me do? Destroy them? Erase them? They are Elves. They are our children."


"I would have you watch them. I would have you guide them. I would have you be ready to act when they go too far."


Solari stood. He walked to the window again.


"I cannot act. I am their father. A father does not destroy his children. He hopes. He prays. He waits."


Valerius stood. He walked to his brother's side.


"I am their father too, Solari. I created them. I shaped them. I gave them life. But I am also the god of oaths. I swore to protect the innocent. If the Elves become a threat to the innocent, I will act."


Solari turned to him. "You would curse my children?"


"I would curse anyone who tries to destroy the world."


They stood in silence. The golden light of the Aurealis flickered.


"Then we must hope," Solari said. "We must hope that they choose the light. We must hope that they listen to reason. We must hope that they remember who they are."


Valerius nodded. "I will hope with you. But I will also prepare."


The two brothers stood at the window. They watched the mortals below. They watched the Elves build their cities. They watched the Dwarves dig their mines. They watched the Orcs fight their wars.


They did not speak again that day. They did not need to.


---


### ONE SHOT SIXTEEN: THE NECROMANCER'S FOLLY


**Era:** The Imperial Period, approximately 2800 AW
**Location:** The Underdark, beneath Vallia
**Character:** Morwen, a human necromancer


Morwen had been a scholar once. She had studied at the Great Library in Aurelia. She had read thousands of books. She had learned hundreds of spells. She had been respected.


Then her daughter died.


A fever. A fast fever. Three days from healthy to corpse. The healers could do nothing. The priests could do nothing. The gods did not answer.


Morwen had tried to accept it. She had tried to grieve. She had tried to move on. She could not.


She began to study necromancy.


The books were forbidden. The spells were taboo. The knowledge was dangerous. She did not care.


She learned how to bind souls. She learned how to raise the dead. She learned how to steal from Vandor's gate.


She knew the risks. She knew the consequences. She did not care.


She found a corpse. Not her daughter's. A stranger's. She needed to practice.


She performed the ritual. She reached through the Aether. She touched Vandor's gate. She pulled a soul back.


The corpse opened its eyes. It sat up. It looked at her.


"Mother?" it said.


It was not her daughter. It was a stranger. A young man who had died in a mining accident. His soul was confused. His mind was broken. He did not know where he was.


Morwen released him. The corpse fell. The soul returned to Vandor.


She had failed. She would try again.


She tried again. And again. And again.


Each time, the corpse spoke. Each time, it called her mother. Each time, she released it.


She could not find her daughter. The soul was too far. The connection was too weak. The gate was too strong.


Vandor was watching.


She felt him. A cold presence. A pressure on her mind. A voice that was not a voice.


"Stop."


She did not stop.


She gathered more power. She performed a stronger ritual. She reached deeper into the gate.


She found her daughter.


"Morwen?" the voice said. "Mother?"


"Elara," Morwen whispered. "I am here. I am coming for you."


She pulled. The soul resisted. The gate resisted. Vandor resisted.


The corpse sat up. It was her daughter. It had her face. Her eyes. Her voice.


"Mother, why?"


"Because I love you."


"I was at peace. I was happy. Why did you bring me back?"


Morwen had no answer.


The corpse crumbled. The soul returned to Vandor. The gate slammed shut.


Vandor came for her.


She did not see him. She felt him. A cold hand on her shoulder. A voice in her ear.


"You were warned."


She tried to run. She could not. She tried to fight. She could not. She tried to beg. She could not.


The darkness swallowed her.


She woke on a battlefield. Naked. Unarmed. Surrounded by hungry things.


Malakor's realm. The endless combat. The eternal death.


She fought. She died. She woke. She fought again.


She would fight forever.


Her daughter was still at peace. Her daughter was still happy. Her daughter was still beyond her reach.


Morwen had saved no one. She had only damned herself.


---


### ONE SHOT SEVENTEEN: THE ORC'S LAST DAY


**Era:** The Destruction of Machaon, approximately 36,180 BA
**Location:** The surface of Machaon, before it collapsed
**Character:** Grok, a Ghorari orc warrior


Grok had been fighting for three hundred years.


He did not remember peace. He had never seen a field that was not churned by boots and soaked with blood. He had never drunk water that did not taste of iron. He had never slept through the night without the screams of the dying echoing in his skull.


Machaon was a world of fire and iron. The sky was red. The ground was black. The volcanoes belched ash that turned the sun into a dim orange disc. The air smelled of sulfur and death.


Grok was a Ghorari. He was born in the mountains. He had been a child when he killed his first enemy. He could not remember the face. He could not remember the name. He remembered the blood on his hands.


He was a warrior. It was all he had ever been.


Malakor walked among them. The god of war was a giant of obsidian and fire. His eyes burned like molten gold. His voice was the grinding of tectonic plates. He did not lead. He did not command. He fought.


Grok had seen Malakor kill a hundred Orcs in a single battle. He had seen him tear through enemy lines like a landslide. He had seen him pick up a warrior by the throat and squeeze until the head came off.


Grok did not hate Malakor. He worshipped him. The god was rage incarnate. The god was strength without limit. The god was everything Grok aspired to be.


The war had no name. There was no enemy to defeat. There was no territory to conquer. There was only the fight. Orcs killed Orcs. Clans killed clans. Brothers killed brothers. The dead were stacked like cordwood. The living fought on.


Grok had lost his father in the first year. He had lost his mother in the tenth. He had lost his brothers and sisters one by one over the centuries. He did not know how many of his children still lived. He had stopped counting.


The ground shook. Not from the volcanoes. From the cracks. The planet was dying.


Grok felt it in his bones. The core was breaking. The crust was shifting. The world was tearing itself apart.


He did not care. He had a battle to fight.


The enemy clan was the Vrakari. They came from the swamps. They were alchemists and poisoners. They did not fight fair. They did not fight clean. They fought to kill.


Grok charged.


His axe was heavy. His armor was thick. His rage was hot.


He cut through the first Vrakari like a scythe through wheat. The body fell in two pieces. Blood sprayed across his face.


The second Vrakari stabbed at his belly. Grok blocked with his forearm. The blade skidded off his bone ridge. He headbutted the enemy. The skull cracked. The body fell.


The third Vrakari was young. Too young. Sixteen years old, perhaps. His tusks were not fully grown. His eyes were wide with terror.


Grok hesitated.


The young Vrakari stabbed him in the thigh.


Grok roared. He swung his axe. The young Vrakari's head left his shoulders. It bounced twice on the black stone and came to rest in a puddle of blood.


Grok looked at the head. The eyes were still open. The mouth was still moving. Forming a word.


"Please."


Grok had heard that word before. He had said it himself, once, when he was young and afraid. He had survived. The boy had not.


The ground shook again. A crack opened beneath his feet. He jumped back. Lava bubbled up from the fissure. The heat was so intense that his armor began to glow.


The Vrakari were retreating. They had seen the cracks. They had felt the shaking. They were running for the tunnels.


Grok did not run. He stood in the middle of the battlefield and watched the world die.


The sky was splitting. Not clouds. The sky itself. The atmosphere was tearing. The red was turning to black.


Malakor appeared beside him. The god was covered in blood. His own blood. He had been wounded. Grok had never seen him wounded.


"It is over," Malakor said.


Grok looked at him. "What is over?"


"The world. Machaon. The Orcs. It is over."


Malakor knelt. He put his hand on Grok's shoulder. His fingers were hot enough to melt iron. Grok did not flinch.


"You fought well," the god said. "You fought harder than any of them. You deserve to know the truth."


Grok waited.


"I am not going to save you. I cannot save everyone. The world is dying. I can only save a few."


Grok nodded. "I know."


"I am taking the survivors to Terra. A new world. A new home. You will not be among them."


Grok had expected this. He had known it for centuries. He was not the strongest. He was not the smartest. He was not the most valuable. He was just a warrior.


"Then I will die here," Grok said.


Malakor stood. "Yes. You will."


The god turned to leave.


"Malakor," Grok said.


The god looked back.


"Do not let them forget. Do not let the survivors forget where they came from. Machaon was a graveyard. But it was our graveyard."


Malakor nodded. He vanished.


The ground split open. The lava rose. The sky fell.


Grok stood in the center of the dying world. He raised his axe. He roared.


The roar was not a challenge. It was not a cry for help. It was a goodbye.


The lava took him first. His legs melted. He did not feel it. His body was already numb.


Then the sky took him. The atmosphere collapsed. The pressure crushed his chest. His ribs broke. His lungs collapsed.


Then the Void took him. Not the Void of non existence. The Void of death. Vandor's gate.


He woke on a battlefield. Naked. Unarmed. Surrounded by hungry things.


Malakor's realm. The endless combat. The eternal death.


He smiled. He had been a warrior in life. He would be a warrior in death.


He picked up a rock. He charged.


---


### ONE SHOT TWENTY: THE BROKEN CROWN


**Era:** The Imperial Period, approximately 2200 AW
**Location:** The Palace of Aurelia, Vallia
**Character:** Emperor Priscus V of House Julius, later known as the Usurper


Priscus had never wanted the throne.


He was the second son. His older brother, Lucius, was the heir. Lucius was everything a future emperor should be. Strong. Charismatic. Wise. Beloved by the Senate, the legions, and the people. Priscus was none of those things. He was quiet. He was awkward. He preferred books to battles, gardens to forums, solitude to crowds.


He loved his brother. He was content to serve. He would have been happy as a provincial governor, a general, a scholar. He would have been happy anywhere but on the throne.


The assassination changed everything.


Lucius was killed on the road to Silverport. A Skaldian raiding party, the official report said. Priscus never believed it. The Skaldians did not use poison. Lucius had been poisoned.


The investigation was brief. The suspects were executed. The case was closed.


Priscus was crowned emperor. He was twenty six years old. He was not ready.


The first year was chaos. The Senate tested him. The legions tested him. The other houses tested him. He passed. Barely. He was not strong, but he was clever. He outmaneuvered his enemies. He survived.


The whispers started in the second year. They said Priscus had killed his brother. They said he had hired the assassins. They said he had coveted the throne all along.


The whispers were not true. Priscus had loved his brother. He would have died for him. He would have killed for him. He had not.


But the whispers did not stop.


In the third year, Priscus made a mistake. He fell in love.


Her name was Valeria. She was a senator's daughter. Beautiful. Intelligent. Ambitious. She saw something in Priscus that no one else saw. She saw the man beneath the crown. She saw the grief. She saw the loneliness. She saw the fear.


They married. The Senate approved. The people cheered. Priscus was happy for the first time since his brother's death.


The whispers stopped. The rumors faded. The empire prospered.


Priscus should have been content.


He was not.


He could not forget the rumors. He could not forget that people had believed him capable of killing his own brother. He could not forget that his enemies had used the accusation against him.


He became paranoid. He saw plots everywhere. He trusted no one. Not the Senate. Not the legions. Not even his wife.


The purge began in the fifth year.


Priscus accused three senators of conspiracy. He had them executed without trial. The Senate was outraged. The people were silent.


He accused five generals of treason. He had them executed without trial. The legions were outraged. The people were afraid.


He accused his own cousin of plotting against him. He had him executed without trial. The family was outraged. The people were terrified.


Valeria confronted him in the throne room.


"Priscus, stop. This is madness. You are killing innocent people."


"They are not innocent. They are conspirators. They want to destroy me."


"No one wants to destroy you. You are destroying yourself."


He struck her.


He had never struck anyone before. He had never raised his hand in anger. He was not a violent man. He was a frightened man.


Valeria left. She did not look back. She divorced him. She returned to her father's house. She never spoke to him again.


Priscus was alone.


The assassination came in the seventh year.


A group of senators, generals, and nobles had finally had enough. They did not poison him. They did not stab him in the dark. They confronted him in the throne room.


"Priscus, you are no longer fit to rule. Step down, or we will remove you."


Priscus looked at them. He saw their faces. He saw the fear. He saw the anger. He saw the pity.


"I did not kill my brother," he said.


"We know," the leader said. "But that does not matter anymore."


They gave him a choice. Exile or death.


He chose exile.


He sailed to the Broken Isles. He lived in a small house on a small island. He grew vegetables. He fished. He read books. He was alone.


He wrote letters to Valeria. She did not reply.


He wrote letters to his children. They did not reply.


He wrote letters to the Senate, begging for forgiveness. They did not reply.


He died ten years later. He was not mourned. He was not remembered. He was the Usurper.


But he had not killed his brother. That was the truth. No one believed him. That was the tragedy.


---


### ONE SHOT TWENTY THREE: THE DEEP


**A Horror Story of Thalassa**


**Era:** The Imperial Period, approximately 4000 AW
**Location:** The Sapphire Expanse, far from any shore
**Character:** Captain Elias Vex, an Oru pirate


**Part One: The Voyage**


The Sea Serpent was not a lucky ship. Elias Vex had known this when he bought her. The wood was old. The caulking was cracked. The crew was superstitious. They refused to sail on certain days. They refused to speak certain words. They threw salt over their shoulders and touched iron and prayed to gods Elias did not believe in.


But the Sea Serpent was cheap. And Elias was desperate.


He had lost his last ship to a Vallian patrol. He had lost his crew to the noose. He had lost his reputation to the whispers. He was not a pirate king anymore. He was a man with a rotting ship and a crew of fools.


The cargo was silk. High quality, low price, stolen from a Qashari merchant who would not miss it. The destination was Port Oru. The route was long, but safe. Stay close to the coast. Avoid the deep waters. Do not sail at night.


Elias knew the rules. He broke them anyway.


"We will save time," he said. "We will cross the deep."


The crew protested. The deep was cursed. The deep was where the Thing lived. The deep was where sailors went mad.


Elias laughed. "I have sailed these waters for forty years. I have never seen a monster. I have never seen a ghost. I have seen men afraid of the dark. That is all."


He gave the order. The Sea Serpent turned away from the coast. The water grew dark. The waves grew still. The air grew cold.


**Part Two: The Change**


The first day was uneventful. The second day was the same. On the third day, the sailors began to whisper.


"The water is wrong," the helmsman said.


"It is water," Elias said. "It is always water."


"It is too still. There are no waves. There are no fish. There is nothing."


Elias looked over the side. The water was black. Not dark blue. Black. Like oil. Like ink. Like something that should not exist.


"We are close to the trench," he said. "The water is deep. That is all."


The helmsman did not answer. He touched his iron pendant. He whispered a prayer to Astraea.


On the fourth day, the fog came.


It was not natural fog. It did not roll in from the sea. It rose from the water itself, thick and cold and smelling of rot. The sailors could not see the bow. They could not see the mast. They could not see each other.


Elias ordered them to stay at their posts. They obeyed. They were afraid. They were good sailors.


The fog lifted on the fifth day. The water was black. The sky was grey. The sun was a pale disc, hidden behind clouds.


And something was in the water.


**Part Three: The Thing**


The first sailor to see it was a boy named Finn. He was seventeen years old, new to the crew, eager to prove himself. He was leaning over the railing, staring into the black water.


He screamed.


Elias ran to him. "What is it?"


The boy pointed. His hand was shaking. His face was white.


Elias looked.


Below the surface, something pale was moving. Not a fish. Not a whale. Something else. Something wrong. It was long and thin and pale, like a drowned corpse. It had too many limbs. Its eyes were black. Its mouth was too wide.


Elias stepped back. His hand went to his cutlass.


The thing disappeared.


"Just a fish," he said. "A deep sea fish. They look strange. That is all."


The boy did not answer. He was staring at the water. His eyes were wide. His mouth was open.


That night, the screaming started.


**Part Four: The First Death**


The cook was the first to die. His name was Gregor. He was a fat man, loud and cheerful, always singing. He went to the galley to prepare the evening meal. He did not return.


The sailors found him in the morning. His body was in the galley. What was left of it.


He had been opened from throat to groin. His organs were spread across the floor. His eyes were gone. His tongue was gone. His face was frozen in a mask of terror.


The sailors vomited. The sailors prayed. The sailors wept.


Elias looked at the body. He did not weep. He did not pray. He looked at the wounds. They were not made by a blade. They were made by teeth. Human teeth. Small, sharp, too many.


"We are leaving," he said. "Turn the ship. Now."


The helmsman tried. The rudder was stuck. He went below to check. He did not return.


They found his body wedged between the rudder and the hull. He had been crushed. His bones were broken. His skull was cracked. His eyes were gone.


The crew panicked. They lowered the lifeboats. Three men climbed in. They rowed away from the ship.


They did not reach the shore. The water churned. The screams came. Then silence.


The lifeboats returned empty.


**Part Five: The Madness**


The Thing did not kill quickly. It killed slowly. One by one.


The carpenter went next. He was found in his bunk, his throat cut from ear to ear. He had done it himself. His knife was still in his hand. His eyes were still open.


The first mate followed. He jumped overboard, screaming about the voices. The crew watched him sink. They did not try to save him.


The cabin boy was found hiding in the hold, curled in a ball, rocking back and forth. He was not dead. He was not alive. He was somewhere else. His eyes were white. His mouth was moving. No sound came out.


"What did you see?" Elias asked.


The boy did not answer.


"What did you see?"


The boy turned his head. His eyes focused on Elias. For a moment, he looked sane.


"She is hungry," he said. "She is always hungry."


Then he stopped breathing. His heart gave out. His body was intact. His mind was gone.


Elias was alone.


**Part Six: The Hunger**


He stood on the deck of the Sea Serpent. The ship was silent. The bodies of his crew lay where they had fallen. He did not bury them. He did not have the strength.


The water was black. The sky was grey. The sun was gone.


He saw her.


She rose from the water. Not quickly. Slowly. Deliberately. She wanted him to see. She wanted him to understand.


She was pale. Translucent. Her skin was the color of drowned things. Her eyes were black, solid black, no iris, no white. Her fingers were too long, tipped with claws. Her mouth was too wide, filled with too many teeth.


She was not a monster. She was a girl. A young girl, thin and small, with wet hair and bare feet.


She smiled.


Elias drew his cutlass. His hand was steady. His heart was not.


"You are not real," he said.


She tilted her head. Her smile widened.


"I am the only real thing in this ocean," she said.


Her voice was not a voice. It was a whisper, a scream, a song, a sob. It was the sound of the deep.


Elias charged.


He swung his cutlass. She caught it with her bare hand. The blade shattered.


She reached out. Her fingers touched his chest. Not hard. Gently. Like a lover.


He felt his heart stop. Not stop beating. Stop existing. There was a hole in his chest where his heart had been. He could see through it.


He fell to his knees.


"Why?" he whispered.


She knelt in front of him. Her black eyes looked into his.


"Because I am hungry," she said. "And you are here."


She ate him. Slowly. Piece by piece. He was conscious for all of it.


The Sea Serpent drifted for days. The Qashari found her. They burned her. They did not report what they found.


Elias Vex was not the first. He was not the last.


Thalassa swam on.


---


### ONE SHOT TWENTY FOUR: THE RED SKY


**A Story of Malakor's Realm**


**Era:** The Kingdom Period, approximately 4500 AW
**Location:** Malakor's Battlegrounds, Hell
**Character:** Gunnar, a damned soul, a former Skaldian warlord


**Part One: The Wake**


Gunnar woke on the black earth. The sky was red. The air smelled of ozone and iron. In the distance, mountains of obsidian rose against a horizon of fire. Lightning crackled in the clouds. Thunder rolled across the plain.


He was naked. He was unarmed. He was not alone.


Bodies lay scattered across the battlefield, thousands of them, some still moving, some already rising. Souls damned to Hell. Souls doomed to fight forever. Men, women, elves, dwarves, orcs. They had all been wicked. They had all been cruel. They had all been sent here to suffer.


Gunnar remembered dying. He had been a Skaldian warlord, feared across the highlands of Nordvorn. He had raided Vallian villages. He had killed priests. He had burned a temple dedicated to Valerius. He had laughed while it burned. He had watched the priest's wife weep. He had not felt pity. He had felt hunger.


Vandor had not sent him to the Great Foundation. He had not earned rest. He had not earned peace. He had earned Hell.


Gunnar smiled. He had been a warrior in life. He would be a warrior in death.


He stood. His muscles ached. His head throbbed. The memory of his death was fresh. A Vallian blade through his chest. He had bled out in the mud, cursing, screaming, refusing to die. He had died anyway.


He looked around. A corpse lay nearby. A man, young, barely more than a boy. He had been dead for minutes. His sword was still in his hand. His eyes were still open. His mouth was still frozen in a scream.


Gunnar took the sword. He tested the weight. It was balanced. It would do. The boy's blood was still warm on the hilt.


A soul approached him. A young man, maybe twenty, with dark hair and terrified eyes. He was holding a broken spear. His hands were shaking. His lips were trembling.


"Please," the boy said. "I do not want to fight. I do not know why I am here. I was not evil. I was not cruel. I stole bread. That is all. I stole bread."


Gunnar looked at him. He did not care.


"I stole bread," the boy said again. "Please. Have mercy."


Gunnar killed him. The sword went through the boy's chest. It was quick. It was clean. It was not merciful.


The boy fell. His blood soaked into the black earth. He would wake tomorrow. He would fight again. He would die again. That was Hell.


Gunnar did not feel guilt. There was no guilt in Hell. There was only the battle. Only the kill. Only the wake.


**Part Two: The Butcher**


Gunnar fought for days. He did not keep count. He killed anyone who came near him. He did not ask questions. He did not offer mercy. He did not hesitate.


A woman with a knife. She was fast. She was skilled. She almost cut his throat. He broke her arm, took her knife, and stabbed her seventeen times. She fell. She would wake tomorrow.


A dwarf with an axe. He was strong. He was stubborn. He swung the axe like a hammer. Gunnar dodged, stepped inside his guard, and drove the sword through his neck. The dwarf fell. He would wake tomorrow.


An elf with a bow. She was fast. She was accurate. She put three arrows in Gunnar's chest before he reached her. He pulled them out. He kept running. He killed her with his bare hands. She fell. She would wake tomorrow.


Gunnar was not the strongest soul in Hell. He was not the fastest. He was the most brutal. He did not fight fair. He did not fight with honor. He fought to kill. He fought to survive. He fought because there was nothing else.


He learned the rules quickly. Death was temporary. Pain was fleeting. Morality was meaningless. There was only the battle. Only the red sky. Only the black earth.


He killed a hundred souls. Then two hundred. Then five hundred. He stopped counting. He did not need to.


The other souls began to whisper. A new killer had arrived. A Skaldian. A butcher. They called him the Red Hand.


Gunnar liked the name. He painted his hand with the blood of his enemies. He did not wash it off. It became his signature. It became his banner.


He killed more. He killed faster. He killed without thought. He was not happy. He was not sad. He was not anything. He was a machine. An engine of death. A red hand in the red sky.


The other souls avoided him. They saw him coming. They ran. He chased them. He killed them anyway. There was no escape. This was Hell.


**Part Three: The Red Hand**


He killed a thousand souls. Then two thousand. Then five thousand.


He stopped counting. He stopped caring. He stopped thinking.


He was the Red Hand. He was the terror of the battlefield. He was the reason souls wept when they saw him coming.


He did not feel pride. He did not feel satisfaction. He felt nothing.


A woman knelt before him. She was old. She was grey. She was weeping.


"I was a healer," she said. "I helped people. I do not know why I am here."


Gunnar killed her. He did not hesitate.


He did not believe her. Everyone in Hell deserved to be here. Vandor did not make mistakes.


The old woman fell. She would wake tomorrow.


Gunnar moved on.


**Part Four: The Thunder**


On his thousandth day, Gunnar heard a sound. Not the clash of steel. Not the screams of the dying. Not the grunt of effort. Not the weeping of the damned.


Thunder.


He looked up. The red sky was clear. There were no clouds. There was no storm. The lightning was distant, flickering on the horizon, harmless.


The thunder came again. Closer. Louder. The ground shook.


The other souls stopped fighting. They looked toward the sound. They were afraid.


Gunnar had never seen them afraid. He had killed them. He had tortured them. He had made them suffer. They had not been afraid. They had been resigned. They had been hopeless. They had not been afraid.


Now they were afraid.


The thunder came again. The ground shook harder. Cracks spread across the black earth. The obsidian mountains trembled.


Gunnar saw him.


A man walked across the battlefield. He was tall, broad, with red hair like fire and golden eyes like embers. He wore no armor. He carried no shield. He carried a hammer. The hammer was glowing. Lightning crackled around its head. Lightning danced across his knuckles. Lightning arced from his fingertips to the ground.


He was not running. He was not hiding. He was walking. Slowly. Deliberately. Like a god. Because he was a god. Not a god. A demigod. The son of Malakor. The Storm-Born. The Giant-Killer. The Thunder of the Fjords.


Thors.


Gunnar had heard stories. Every Skaldian knew the stories. The Storm-Born who killed the World Serpent. The hero who died to save the world. The demigod who fought beside Malakor in the red sky.


Gunnar had not believed the stories. He believed them now.


The souls fled. They ran in every direction. They dropped their weapons. They screamed. They begged. They prayed.


Thors raised his hammer. Lightning exploded outward. A wave of electricity swept across the battlefield. Bodies flew. Bodies burned. Bodies fell. The smell of ozone drowned the smell of iron.


Gunnar watched. He did not run. He could not run. His legs would not move. His hands would not lift his sword. His heart would not beat.


Thors saw him. He walked toward him. The other souls scattered. They did not want to be near the Red Hand. They did not want to be near Thors.


Gunnar stood his ground. He had killed five thousand souls. He was not afraid of anything. He was not afraid of death. He was not afraid of pain. He was not afraid of this red-haired giant with the glowing hammer.


Thors stopped ten paces away. He looked at Gunnar. His golden eyes were cold. They were not cruel. They were not kind. They were the eyes of a man who had killed millions.


"You are not running," Thors said.


His voice was calm. It was deep. It was the voice of thunder rolling across the mountains.


"I am not," Gunnar said. His voice did not shake. He was proud of that.


"You should be."


"I have killed five thousand souls in this realm. I am the Red Hand. I am not afraid of you."


Thors smiled. It was not a kind smile. It was the smile of a wolf. The smile of a storm. The smile of a man who had fought the World Serpent and won.


"I am Thors," he said. "I have killed millions."


He raised his hammer. Lightning gathered around its head. The air crackled. The ground trembled. The sky darkened.


Gunnar raised his sword. It felt like a toy. It felt like a twig. It felt like nothing.


"I am the Red Hand," Gunnar said again. He did not know why. He had nothing else to say.


Thors did not answer. He swung the hammer.


**Part Five: The Death**


The lightning struck Gunnar in the chest. He did not see it. He did not hear it. He felt it. For a fraction of a second, he was fire. He was light. He was nothing.


He flew backward. Fifty yards. A hundred. He did not know. He could not see. He could not feel. He was blind. He was numb. He was dead.


He landed in a heap. His body was smoking. His skin was cracked. His ribs were broken. His organs were cooked. His eyes were gone. Burned out of his skull.


He was not dead. He could not die. He would wake tomorrow.


He heard footsteps. Thors walked toward him. The ground shook with each step.


Thors stood over him. Gunnar could not see him. He could feel him. The heat. The pressure. The weight of a demigod's presence.


"Tomorrow," Thors said, "you will run."


He raised Mjölnir. He brought it down on Gunnar's head.


Darkness.


**Part Six: The Wake**


Gunnar woke on the black earth. The sky was red. The air smelled of ozone and iron.


He was naked. He was unarmed. He was alone.


He remembered dying. He remembered Thors. He remembered the lightning. He remembered the hammer.


He stood. His body was whole. His eyes were back. His skin was smooth. His ribs were healed.


He looked around. A corpse lay nearby. A woman with a sword. He took it.


He did not run. He would not run. He was a Skaldian. He was a warlord. He was the Red Hand.


He would find Thors. He would try again.


He would die again. He would wake again.


This was Hell. There was no escape.


Gunnar walked across the battlefield. The other souls avoided him. They knew his name. They knew his hand. They knew his reputation.


He did not care. He was looking for the storm. He was looking for the thunder. He was looking for the red-haired giant with the glowing hammer.


He would find him. He would fight him. He would lose. He would wake. He would try again.


That was Hell.


That was the red sky.


---


## PART SIX: DEMIGOD STORIES


---


### ONE SHOT TWENTY FIVE: THE WITNESS


**A Story of Malakor's Realm**


**Era:** 800 AW, the first meeting between Aurelian and Thors
**Location:** Malakor's Battlegrounds, Hell
**Character:** Erika, a damned soul, a former Qashari mage


**Part One: The Damned**


Erika had been in Hell for three hundred years.


She did not remember her life clearly. Faces faded. Names slipped away. She remembered sand. She remembered heat. She remembered a man with a kind smile and a knife. She remembered the knife. She did not remember the rest. Her mind had protected her. Hell had eroded everything else.


She was a damned soul. She was not a warrior. She was not a fighter. She had been a scholar, once, a long time ago. Now she was prey.


She woke on the black earth. The sky was red. The air smelled of ozone and iron. Bodies lay scattered around her. Some were already rising. Some were still. Some would never rise again. They would wake tomorrow.


Erika stood. Her body ached. Her head throbbed. She had been killed yesterday. She could not remember how. She did not want to.


She looked around. A sword lay nearby. The owner was dead. She picked it up. The weight was wrong. The balance was off. She did not care. She needed something to defend herself.


She walked. She did not know where she was going. She walked away from the fighting. She always walked away from the fighting.


She was a coward. She knew it. She did not care.


**Part Two: The Quiet**


Erika found a cleft in the obsidian mountains. A narrow gap, barely wide enough for her body. She squeezed inside. The walls were sharp. They cut her arms. She did not feel it.


The cleft opened into a small cave. The ceiling was low. The floor was black glass. There was no light except the faint red glow from the sky, filtering through the crack.


She sat. She leaned against the wall. She closed her eyes.


She did not sleep. She could not sleep. The damned did not sleep. They rested. They waited. They dreaded the next battle.


A sound echoed through the mountain. Not thunder. Not a scream. Something else. A crack. A boom. A roar.


Erika opened her eyes. The ground shook. Small pieces of obsidian fell from the ceiling.


She stood. She moved to the entrance of the cleft. She looked out.


The battlefield was empty. The damned had fled. They had run to the edges of the realm, to the mountains, to the tunnels. Something had scared them.


Erika had never seen them run. They fought. They died. They woke. They fought again. They did not run.


She stepped out of the cleft. She walked toward the center of the battlefield. She did not know why.


**Part Three: The Light**


She saw them from a distance. Two figures. One golden. One red.


They stood across from each other. The golden one was tall, calm, radiant. He wore robes of white and gold. He held a sword made of light. He was not afraid.


The red one was massive, broad, wild. His hair was like fire. His eyes were like embers. He held a hammer that crackled with lightning. He was not afraid either.


Erika had never seen the red one before. She had seen many souls in Hell. She had never seen him.


She knelt behind a boulder. She watched.


The golden one spoke. She could not hear the words. The wind carried them away.


The red one laughed. It was not a cruel laugh. It was not a happy laugh. It was the laugh of a warrior who had found what he was looking for.


They raised their weapons.


Erika held her breath.


**Part Four: The Storm**


They fought.


The golden one moved like light. His sword was a blur. He was fast. He was precise. He was beautiful.


The red one moved like thunder. His hammer was a storm. He was strong. He was relentless. He was terrifying.


The ground shook. The sky darkened. Lightning and light collided. The air crackled. The earth cracked.


Erika had never seen anything like this. She had watched battles. She had died in battles. She had been killed by warriors, by cowards, by monsters. She had never seen gods fight.


Not gods. Demigods.


She did not know their names. She did not need to.


The red one swung his hammer. The golden one dodged. The hammer struck the ground. Lightning exploded outward. The boulder in front of Erika shattered. She screamed. She covered her head.


The fighting stopped.


She looked up.


The golden one was looking at her.


**Part Five: The Eyes**


His eyes were gold. Not like the red one's eyes. Not burning. Not fierce. Calm. Warm. Like the sun.


He did not speak. He did not move. He looked at her.


Erika could not move. She could not breathe. She could not look away.


He nodded.


Then he turned back to the red one.


They fought again.


Erika watched. She did not run. She did not hide. She watched.


The golden one bled. The red one bled. They did not stop.


Hours passed. Maybe days. Time did not exist in Hell.


The red one swung wide. The golden one stepped inside. His sword pierced the red one's side.


The red one fell. His hammer dropped. He laughed.


The golden one lowered his sword.


"It is over," he said.


The red one picked up his hammer. He stood. He raised it in salute.


"You are the first person to beat me," he said.


"You are the first person to push me this far since I died," the golden one said.


They sat on the black earth. They talked. They laughed. They were not enemies. They were not friends. They were something else.


Erika did not understand. She did not need to.


**Part Six: The Departure**


The golden one stood.


"I should go," he said.


"Will you come back?" the red one asked.


"Yes. I want a rematch."


The red one smiled. "Then I will be here."


The golden one walked across the battlefield. He walked toward the grey light in the distance. He did not look back.


The red one watched him go. He raised his hammer in salute.


Then he turned. He looked at Erika.


She flinched. She had been seen.


He walked toward her. She did not run. She could not.


He stopped in front of her. He was tall. He was covered in blood. His eyes were gold.


"You saw," he said.


"Yes."


"Tell no one."


"I have no one to tell."


He looked at her. His eyes softened. Just a little.


"What is your name?" he asked.


"Erika."


"I am Thors."


"I know."


He nodded. He turned. He walked away.


Erika watched him go. She did not move. She did not speak. She watched.


The red sky burned. The black earth smoked.


She was still in Hell. She was still damned. She was still afraid.


But she had seen something. Something she would not forget.


The golden one. The red one. The fight. The laughter.


She would not tell anyone. There was no one to tell.


But she would remember.


**Part Seven: The Aftermath**


Erika returned to her cleft. She sat in the darkness. She closed her eyes.


She did not sleep. She never slept.


But she dreamed. She dreamed of light and lightning. She dreamed of a golden figure and a red figure. She dreamed of a fight that was not a battle. It was something else.


She did not understand it. She did not need to.


She was a damned soul. She was a coward. She was nothing.


But she had seen something beautiful.


That was enough.


---


### ONE SHOT TWENTY TWO: THE BROTHERS O'CONNOR


**A Skaldian Legend of the Highland Clans**


**Era:** The Kingdom Period, approximately 4900 AW
**Location:** The Highland Clans territory, Nordvorn
**Characters:** Sean O'Connor and Rían O'Connor, brothers of House O'Connor


**Part One: The Gifts**


The skalds sing of many heroes, but they sing of the O'Connor brothers most of all.


Sean was the elder. He was born with a sword in his hand, or so the stories say. He moved like water, like wind, like something that had never known effort. He was tall and fair, with hands that never seemed to touch the blade, only guide it. The clan loved him without him having to ask. Their father looked at Sean and saw himself, only better. He was the heir.


Rían was the younger. He was born with a hunger that had no name. He was shorter than his brother, darker, with shoulders thickened by a thousand hours of practice. He broke three swords learning to parry. He shattered two shields learning to block. He woke before dawn and slept after midnight. The clan acknowledged him only as Sean's brother. Their father looked at Rían and saw a boy who would never be good enough.


They loved each other. That was the only thing that saved them.


**Part Two: The Boys**


As children, they were inseparable. Sean taught Rían to fish. Rían taught Sean to track deer. They built a hidden fort in the pine woods, a circle of stones and branches where no one else was allowed. They swore blood oaths on their mother's grave. They promised to always protect each other.


But the cracks appeared early.


Sean won the foot race at the spring festival. Rían came second.


Sean killed his first elk at twelve. Rían killed his first elk at fifteen.


Sean was named his father's heir at sixteen. Rían was named his father's spare.


Their father was not a cruel man. He simply did not see Rían. He saw Sean. He saw the heir. He saw the future. Rían was the shadow.


Rían did not resent Sean. He resented himself. He worked harder. He trained longer. He wanted to be as good as Sean. He wanted to be better.


Sean did not understand. He did not have to work. Things came to him. He did not know why Rían was always so tired, always so angry.


"I am not angry," Rían said. "I am tired of being your shadow."


"Then step out of it," Sean said. "No one is stopping you."


That was the first time they almost fought. Rían's hand went to his knife. Sean's hand went to his sword. Their father stepped between them.


"Enough."


They lowered their hands. They did not speak for a week.


**Part Three: The Raid**


The O'Sullivans had stolen cattle. Not many. A dozen head, maybe. Enough to insult. Enough to demand blood.


Sean led the retaliation. He was twenty five, already called the finest warrior of his generation. Rían went with him. He was twenty two. He had been fighting since he could hold a wooden sword. He had never stopped proving himself.


The raid was swift. The O'Sullivans were caught by surprise. The cattle were recovered. Sean ordered the men to pull back.


Then Rían saw a figure crouched behind a granite outcropping.


The figure was young. Seventeen, maybe. He wore the colors of the O'Sullivans. He was not running. He was not fighting. He was frozen.


Rían hesitated. The boy raised his hands.


An arrow whistled past Rían's ear. Not from the boy. From the ridge above. The O'Sullivans had left a rearguard.


Rían moved. He did not think. He lunged forward, grabbed the boy by the collar, and dragged him behind the outcropping. The boy screamed. Rían's knife was at his throat.


"Stay down or you die," Rían said.


The boy stayed down.


The rearguard fled. The raid was over. Rían released the boy. The boy ran.


Sean arrived a moment later. He had seen everything.


"You saved him," Sean said.


"He was unarmed. He was scared. He was a boy."


"You could have killed him."


"I could have. I did not."


Sean put his hand on Rían's shoulder. "That is why you are my brother."


They rode home together. The skalds would sing of this raid. They would sing of Sean's leadership and Rían's mercy. They would sing of brothers who fought side by side.


That was the last good day.


**Part Four: The Mistake**


Three months later, the O'Sullivans raided back.


They came at night. They burned a granary. They killed two men. They vanished into the mist before anyone could rally.


The O'Connor clan was outraged. They demanded blood. Sean was sent to deliver it.


Rían went with him.


The second raid was not swift. The O'Sullivans were ready. They had set an ambush in a narrow valley. Archers on the ridges. Spearmen in the tall grass. Sean walked into it.


The fighting was chaos. Men screamed. Steel rang against steel. Horses shrieked.


Rían fought like he always fought. Hard. Relentless. He killed a spearman with a thrust to the throat. He killed an archer with a thrown axe. He killed a swordsman who tried to flank Sean.


Then he saw a man in O'Sullivan colors, running toward Sean from behind. The man had a sword. Rían did not hesitate. He intercepted. He drove his blade through the man's chest.


The man fell. Rían saw his face.


It was the boy. The one he had spared. The one he had dragged behind the outcropping. The one who had raised his hands.


The boy was seventeen. He was holding a sword. He was running toward Sean. That was true. But his eyes were wide. His mouth was open. He was not attacking. He was running away. He had been running away. He had not seen Sean. He had been trying to escape.


Rían saw all of this in the space between heartbeats. He saw the boy's face. He saw the sword, still clean. He saw his own blade buried in the boy's chest.


He pulled it out. The boy fell.


Sean arrived a moment later. He looked at the body. He looked at Rían.


"He was running," Sean said.


"I did not see."


"He was running away."


"I did not see."


Sean knelt beside the boy. He closed his eyes. He stood. His face was stone.


"We will speak of this at home," Sean said.


Rían said nothing. His hands were red. He could not stop looking at them.


**Part Five: The Judgment**


Their father was an old man with a cold heart. He had never loved his sons the way other fathers loved theirs. He loved them for what they could do. Sean could lead. Rían could fight. That was enough for him.


He listened to Sean's report. He looked at Rían.


"You killed an unarmed boy."


"He was not unarmed. He had a sword."


"He was running away."


"I did not see."


The old man was silent for a long time. The clan council watched. The warriors watched. Rían stood in the center of the hall, blood still on his hands, and waited.


"The boy was an enemy," the old man said. "He was in the valley. He was armed. Rían made a mistake. But it was a mistake of war, not of malice."


The clan grumbled. They wanted blood. They wanted Rían punished.


"Rían will be flogged," the old man said. "Ten lashes. He will pay weregild to the boy's family. And he will be stripped of his rank for one year."


Rían nodded. He did not argue. He did not beg.


Sean stepped forward. "I will take the lashes for him."


The hall went silent.


"No," Rían said.


"I am the leader of the raid. The responsibility is mine."


"You did not kill him."


"I should have seen. I should have been there."


"Sean, no."


Their father raised his hand. "Sean will take five lashes. Rían will take five. That is my judgment."


They took the lashes together. Side by side. Kneeling in the dirt. The whip tore their backs open. They did not scream. They did not cry. They bled.


Afterward, Rían helped Sean to his feet. Sean helped Rían to his feet. They did not speak.


**Part Six: The Silence**


The shame did not leave Rían. It settled into his bones like a cold sickness. He trained harder. He woke earlier. He slept later. He broke four more swords. He shattered three more shields. He drilled until his hands bled and his arms shook.


Sean watched. He did not know what to say. He had never been good with words. He had always led by example.


The silence grew between them like a crack in ice. It started thin. It spread.


They still fought together. They still trained together. They still ate at the same table. But they did not talk. Not about the boy. Not about the raid. Not about anything that mattered.


Rían dreamed of the boy's face every night. He saw the wide eyes. The open mouth. The clean sword. He woke screaming. Sean heard him through the wall. Sean said nothing.


**Part Seven: The Prisoner**


Two years later, the Fjord Clans raided the highlands.


They came at dawn, in longships, up the river that no one thought they could navigate. They burned two villages before the O'Connors could rally.


Rían was captured. He had been training alone at the highland pass, the place where he and Sean used to sit as boys. He had gone there to think. He had gone there to be alone. The Fjords found him.


They took him alive. They wanted him alive. He was Sean's brother. He was worth more breathing.


Rían did not go quietly. He killed three of them before they brought him down. A spear through his calf. A club to his head. He woke in chains.


The Fjords took him to Ironkeep, a fortress carved into a seaside cliff. No one had ever escaped.


Sean learned of the capture an hour after it happened. He stood up. He walked to the armory. He put on his armor. He chose his sword.


"You cannot go alone," his father said.


"I am not going alone. I am taking my best men."


"Rían made his choices. He killed that boy. The clan has not forgotten."


"I have not forgotten either," Sean said. "He is still my brother."


He gathered twelve men. The best of the O'Connor warriors. They rode east, toward the sea.


**Part Eight: The Rescue**


The fortress was called Ironkeep. The walls were fifteen feet thick. The gates were reinforced with iron bands. The garrison was a hundred strong. No one had ever breached it.


Sean did not care.


He studied the fortress for two days. He watched the patrols. He counted the guards. He noted the blind spots.


The cliff was the weakness. The seaward side was sheer rock, two hundred feet down to the waves. No one watched it. No one could climb it.


Sean climbed it.


He went alone. He left his men at the base of the cliff, with orders to create a diversion at the main gate when they saw his signal.


The climb took three hours. His fingers bled. His arms screamed. The wind nearly tore him off the rock twice. He did not stop.


He reached the top. He pulled himself over the edge. He lay in the shadows, catching his breath.


Rían was in a cell at the top of the keep. Sean found him by following the sound of his brother's breathing. Rían was chanting. A prayer. A mantra. The same words, over and over.


"Stay down. Stay down. Stay down."


Sean picked the lock. The pins clicked. The door opened.


Rían looked up. His face was bruised. His nose was broken. His left arm was bent at an unnatural angle. His eyes were swollen almost shut.


"You came," Rían said. His voice was hoarse.


"You are my brother."


"I killed that boy. I saw his face every night. I still see it."


"I know."


"I am sorry."


"I know."


Sean knelt beside him. He wrapped his arm around Rían's waist. He lifted him to his feet.


"Can you walk?"


"I can crawl if I have to."


"Can you climb?"


Rían looked at the window. The drop was sheer. The sea was below.


"I can hold onto you."


"That is enough."


**Part Nine: The Fall**


They climbed out the window. The wind was stronger here. The spray from the waves made the rocks slick.


Rían could not use his left arm. He wrapped his right arm around Sean's neck. Sean held him with one hand and clung to the rock with the other.


"Let go," Rían said. "I am slowing you down."


"Shut up."


"If you fall, we both die."


"Then we both die."


They climbed. Down. Down. Down. The sea roared below them.


An arrow struck the rock a foot from Sean's head. A guard had seen them.


Another arrow. Another.


Sean let go.


They fell. The wind screamed. The waves rushed up to meet them.


The water was cold. Colder than anything Sean had ever felt. It stole his breath. It numbed his limbs.


He held onto Rían. He kicked for the surface. He broke through. He gasped.


Rían was not moving.


Sean dragged him to shore. His men found them at dawn. They were half a mile down the coast, washed up on a rocky beach. Sean was shivering. Rían was barely breathing.


"He needs a healer," Sean said.


"His arm is broken. His ribs are cracked. He may not survive the ride."


"Then ride faster."


**Part Ten: The Fever**


Rían did not wake for three days. He burned with fever. He thrashed in his sleep. He called out for their mother, who had been dead for fifteen years. He called out for the boy he had killed.


Sean did not leave his side. He changed the bandages. He forced water down his throat. He talked to him, even though Rían could not hear.


"I am sorry too," Sean said. "I should have stood with you. I should have told the clan that I would have done the same thing. I should have told them that any man can make a mistake."


Rían did not answer.


"I was scared. I was the heir. I thought I had to be perfect. I thought I could not show weakness. I was wrong."


Rían's hand twitched.


"I am not going to be chieftain if it means losing you. I will walk away. I will give it to you. I do not care."


Rían's eyes opened. They were red. They were wet.


"You are a liar," Rían whispered. "You love being chieftain."


"Not more than I love you."


Rían laughed. It hurt him. He winced. He smiled.


"We are both liars."


"Yes. We are."


**Part Eleven: The Return**


Rían healed. Slowly. His arm never straightened completely. His ribs ached when it rained. He walked with a limp.


He could still fight. Not like before. Not like Sean. But he could still kill men who needed killing.


The clan watched him differently now. They did not call him the Butcher anymore. They called him the Survivor. They did not sing of his skill with a blade. They sang of his will to live.


Sean became chieftain when their father died. His first act was to name Rían as his war chief.


The clan council objected. "He is crippled," they said. "He cannot lead warriors."


"He is my brother," Sean said. "He has earned the right to stand at my side. If you do not like it, you can leave."


No one left.


Rían served as war chief for twenty years. He did not lead charges. He did not fight duels. He planned. He trained. He taught the young warriors the things he had learned the hard way.


"Do not kill a man who is running away," he told them. "You will see his face every night for the rest of your life."


They listened. They did not understand. Not yet. They would.


**Part Twelve: The End**


They died within a month of each other. Old. Grey. Tired.


Rían went first. Quietly. In his sleep. The limp had become a cane. The cane had become a chair. The chair had become a bed.


Sean followed not long after. He did not die of grief. He died because he was ready. He had done everything he needed to do. The clan was strong. The highlands were at peace. His brother was waiting.


They were buried side by side on the hill overlooking the valley, next to their mother's grave.


The skalds sang at their funeral. They sang of Sean, the natural, the gifted, the warrior who moved like water. They sang of Rían, the grinder, the survivor, the warrior who never quit. They sang of the Ironkeep rescue. They sang of the climb. They sang of the fall.


They did not sing of the boy. That song was not for them.


The headstone reads: Sean and Rían O'Connor. Brothers. Warriors.


---


I have organized the complete tale of Thors Storm-Born into a single, clean document for you to copy and paste. I have kept every word you wrote, added clear separators, and structured it into two parts: the full narrative and the cult appendix.


---


# THORS STORM-BORN


## A Demigod of Malakor, Born of Storm and Battle


**Era:** The Early Vallian Empire, approximately 200 AW to 500 AW
**Parent God:** Malakor
**Mortal Parent:** Helga Iron-Hand, a Skaldian shieldmaiden of House Skjöldung
**Name:** Thors, called Storm-Born, the Giant-Killer, the Thunder of the Fjords, the World Serpent's Bane
**Weapon:** Mjölnir, the Hammer of Storms


---


## PART ONE: HELGA IRON-HAND


Helga was not born a hero. She became one.


She was the daughter of a fisherman, born in a small village on the western fjords of Nordvorn. Her father lost his hands in a fishing accident when she was twelve. He could not work. Her mother had died years before. Helga became the provider.


She learned to fish. She learned to hunt. She learned to fight. When raiders came, she fought them off with a broken oar. When the village elders thanked her, she said, "I did what needed to be done."


She earned her name, Iron-Hand, not because she lost her hands like her father. Because her grip was unbreakable. Once she held onto something, she never let go.


She became a shieldmaiden. She joined the raids. She killed her first enemy at sixteen. She killed her twentieth at twenty. She never lost a duel. She never surrendered.


She did not pray to Malakor. She did not need to. She lived his creed. Strength. Courage. Survival.


---


## PART TWO: THE STORM


One night, a storm struck her village. Not an ordinary storm. The lightning was too bright. The thunder was too loud. The wind howled with a voice that was almost words.


Helga walked to the shore. She stood on the rocks, facing the sea. The rain soaked her. The wind tore at her clothes. She did not flinch.


A figure emerged from the waves. Tall. Broad. Eyes like molten gold. Hair like wildfire. Lightning crackled around him.


"Malakor," she said.


"You know me."


"I know your works."


He stepped onto the shore. The storm raged around them. Lightning struck the water. Thunder shook the cliffs.


"You are the strongest of your people," he said.


"Yes."


"You do not pray to me."


"I do not need to."


He smiled. It was not a kind smile. It was the smile of a wolf who has found a worthy opponent.


"I will give you a child," he said. "A son. He will be stronger than any man. He will command the lightning. He will protect your people. But he will not live forever. He will die young, in a battle that will shake the foundations of the world. His death will save countless lives. In return, you will teach him to be human."


Helga looked at him. She did not kneel. She did not thank him. She did not weep.


"I will teach him to be Skaldian," she said. "That is better."


Malakor laughed. The thunder laughed with him.


---


## PART THREE: THE BIRTH


Thors was born in the winter of 200 AW, during a blizzard that buried the fjords in snow. Lightning flashed through the falling snow. Thunder shook the longhouse walls. The midwives said they had never seen a child so large.


He came into the world with his fists clenched, and sparks danced on his fingertips. His eyes were molten gold, like his father's. His hair was red like fire. His skin was warm to the touch.


He did not cry. He roared.


Helga held him. The lightning outside stopped. The thunder fell silent. The snow continued to fall.


"He will be trouble," the midwives whispered.


"He will be a legend," Helga said.


She named him Thors, an old Skaldian name meaning thunder.


---


## PART FOUR: THE BOY


Thors grew fast. Too fast. He was taller than boys twice his age. He was stronger than men. He had a temper that could shake the walls.


His lightning abilities manifested early. When he was angry, sparks flew from his hands. When he was happy, the air around him hummed. When he was sad, the sky darkened over the village.


He broke his first sword at seven. He broke his second at eight. He broke his third at nine. Helga stopped giving him swords. She made him fight with his hands.


He fought the village boys. He fought the village men. He fought the village elders. He never lost. Not because he was cruel. Because he did not know how to stop.


"You must learn control," Helga said.


"Why?"


"Because strength without control is not strength. It is a storm that destroys everything, including the people you love."


Thors did not understand. He was a child. But he remembered.


She made him practice his lightning. Hour after hour. Day after day. He learned to call it. He learned to quiet it. He learned to aim it.


By the time he was twelve, he could summon a bolt of lightning that could split an oak tree in half.


---


## PART FIVE: THE GIANT


When Thors was sixteen, a giant came down from the mountains of Nordvorn.


Not a metaphor. A real giant. A creature of stone and frost, twelve feet tall, with teeth like daggers and hands like boulders. It had been terrorizing the highlands for months, killing cattle, crushing huts, eating anyone who came too close. The Skaldians called it the Frost Eater.


The Fjord Clans sent warriors. The giant killed them. The Highland Clans sent champions. The giant killed them. The Lowland Clans sent their best knights. The giant killed them.


Thors went alone.


Helga tried to stop him. He would not listen.


"I am my father's son," he said. "I am a child of Malakor. I am the storm."


He climbed the mountain. He found the giant's cave. He walked inside.


The giant laughed. "Little man," it said. "Little, little man."


Thors raised his hand. Lightning answered. It struck the giant in the chest. The giant staggered. It did not fall.


They fought for three days. The mountain shook. The sky darkened. Lightning split the clouds. Thunder rolled across the valleys. The villagers heard the roars and the crashes and the screams.


On the third day, Thors emerged from the cave. He was covered in blood. His own blood. The giant's blood. He was carrying the giant's head. His body crackled with residual lightning. His eyes glowed gold.


He threw the head down the mountain. It bounced for miles. Where it landed, the grass never grew again.


The villagers cheered. They called him Storm-Born. They called him Giant-Killer. They called him the Thunder of the Fjords.


Thors did not smile. He went home. He slept for two days.


---


## PART SIX: MJÖLNIR


When Thors was twenty, he sailed south to the Central Mountains of Vallia. He sought the dwarves of Karaz Stein, the greatest smiths in the world. He asked them to forge him a weapon worthy of a demigod.
